reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
from transformers import AutoConfig, AutoModelForCausalLM


class Model(torch.nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, model_name, config):
        super().__init__()
        self.model_name = model_name
        self.config = config
        self.model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(self.model_name, config=self.config)

    def forward(self, x):
        return self.model(x).logits


model_name = "bigcode/starcoder2-3b"
config = AutoConfig.from_pretrained(model_name)
vocab_size = config.vocab_size
sequence_length = 512
batch_size = 4


def get_inputs():
    inputs = torch.randint(0, vocab_size, (batch_size, sequence_length))
    return [inputs]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [model_name, config]

We need to accelerate the forward pass of a causal language model, specifically `bigcode/starcoder2-3b`. This is a big model with a standard decoder stack. We cannot simply replace the entire forward pass with a fused kernel in one go; instead, we need to write a custom CUDA kernel that replaces some specific operation(s) constituting a significant portion of the model's runtime. The challenge is that the `Model.forward` currently calls `self.model(x).logits`. We need a `ModelNew` that wraps the model but replaces a specific subcomponent. Total 7.4/156 = 47ms per forward. In practice, it'll be maybe 100-200ms. Each MLP layer ~12ms. A fused MLP kernel that does `gelu(xW1+b1)W2+b2` in one go can keep intermediate in registers/shared memory, reducing HBM traffic. The kernel would need to implement GEMM. Writing an efficient GEMM is extremely difficult. We could tile it: each block computes a tile of output. But beating cuBLAS is hard. Wait, let's reconsider the model. `bigcode/starcoder2-3b`. Is there any possibility to use a much simpler kernel to get speedup? The model consists of `nn.Embedding`, then `N` transformer layers, then final `LayerNorm`, then `lm_head`. The `lm_head` is not tied to input embeddings? In GPT-like models it is, but in StarCoder2 it might be separate. The `forward` returns `logits`. The `lm_head` is a linear from `hidden_size` to `vocab_size`. `vocab_size` for starcoder2 is `49152`. So `lm_head` is `[B*S, H] @ [H, V]`. `batch=4`, `seq=512` -> `2048 x 3072 @ 3072 x 49152`. FLOPs: `2048 * 49152 * 3072 * 2 ≈ 618 GFLOPs`. This is one large GEMM. cuBLAS will handle it very well. We are unlikely to beat it with a naive custom kernel. What about the embedding lookup? That's a gather operation. Usually fast enough. Could we replace the `nn.Embedding` with a custom kernel? Probably not worth it. What about replacing the final logits computation? Again, it's a single GEMM. The biggest opportunity is replacing the attention mechanism with a fused kernel, because the reference computes it with multiple memory-bound kernels (matmul -> scale -> mask -> softmax -> dropout -> matmul). On a 512 sequence, we can write a single kernel that loads Q, K, V into shared memory, computes online softmax, and writes the final output. Let's examine the `transformers` implementation of `Starcoder2Attention`: ```python class Starcoder2Attention(nn.Module): def __init__(self, config, layer_idx): ... self.num_heads = config.num_attention_heads ... self.q_proj = nn.Linear(config.hidden_size, self.num_heads * self.head_dim, bias=True) self.k_proj = nn.Linear(config.hidden_size, self.num_key_value_heads * self.head_dim, bias=True) self.v_proj = nn.Linear(config.hidden_size, self.num_key_value_heads * self.head_dim, bias=True) self.o_proj = nn.Linear(self.num_heads * self.head_dim, config.hidden_size, bias=False) ... ``` Forward usually does: ```python def forward(self, hidden_states, ...): bsz, q_len, _ = hidden_states.size() query_states = self.q_proj(hidden_states).view(bsz, q_len, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1,2) key_states = self.k_proj(hidden_states).view(bsz, q_len, self.num_key_value_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1,2) value_states = self.v_proj(hidden_states).view(bsz, q_len, self.num_key_value_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1,2) attn_weights = torch.matmul(query_states, key_states.transpose(-1,-2)) / math.sqrt(self.head_dim) attn_weights = nn.functional.softmax(attn_weights + causal_mask, dim=-1) attn_output = torch.matmul(attn_weights, value_states) attn_output = attn_output.transpose(1,2).contiguous() attn_output = attn_output.reshape(bsz, q_len, self.hidden_size) attn_output = self.o_proj(attn_output) return attn_output ``` This is many kernel launches: q_proj, k_proj, v_proj (3 GEMMs), reshape, transpose, matmul (Q@K^T), scale, add mask, softmax, matmul (attn@V), transpose, reshape, o_proj. A custom kernel that computes: 1. Q, K, V projections (GEMMs) - we can keep these as `nn.Linear` if we want, but fusing them into the attention is even better. 2. Fused attention: `softmax((Q K^T)/sqrt(d) + mask) V`. 3. O projection. Even fusing just step 2 (the attention math) while keeping QKV and O as linear layers will save a lot of memory traffic and kernel launch overhead. But can we beat the reference if the reference uses SDPA? If `transformers` is configured to use SDPA (which is likely in `transformers>=4.36` on PyTorch 2.0+), then the attention part (Q@K^T, softmax, @V) is already fused into one efficient kernel (FlashAttention or memory-efficient attention). If so, the bottleneck shifts to the QKV and O projections. In that case, a custom kernel that fuses QKV projection + attention + O projection would be amazing. But writing a full fused attention with GEMMs is very hard. Let's reconsider: The prompt says "Replace the forward computation of the PyTorch model below with a custom CUDA kernel." Not "replace a part of it". It says `ModelNew.forward()` must implement the forward computation. It does *not* say we must call `self.model(x).logits` internally. So we could reimplement the whole model forward in CUDA. But that is monstrous for a 3B parameter model. However, we can replace specific submodules with our custom wrappers. For example, `ModelNew` could load the pretrained weights, then in `forward`, run all layers *except* the attention via the original modules, and for the attention part call our custom kernel. But monkey-patching is standard. Alternatively, we can use the original model but replace the `forward` of `Starcoder2Attention` with a wrapper that calls the original QKV and O projections, but uses a custom kernel for the attention core (softmax, etc.). If the original already uses SDPA, the custom kernel for the core might not help. If the original uses eager attention, it will help. Since we don't know if SDPA is used, the safest is to write a custom kernel for the core attention, which we know is expensive in eager mode. To make it faster than SDPA, we need it to be at least as efficient as FlashAttention for 512 seq length. This is very possible because 512 is small; we can write a simple shared-memory kernel. Let's plan a custom attention kernel. Parameters: - `B`: batch size (4) - `S`: sequence length (512) - `H`: num attention heads (let's call it `num_heads`) - `Hkv`: num key/value heads (`num_key_value_heads`) - `D`: head dim We need to monkey patch `Starcoder2Attention.forward`. Since `ModelNew` will contain the loaded model, we can patch in `__init__`. The custom kernel takes Q, K, V and computes: `O = causal_attention(Q, K, V)` Input shapes: - Q: `[B, H, S, D]` - K: `[B, Hkv, S, D]` - V: `[B, Hkv, S, D]` Output shape: - O: `[B, H, S, D]` For GQA, each query head `h` uses K/V head `h // (H / Hkv)`. We need to write a CUDA kernel. On an A100 (the likely GPU), Shared Memory per SM is 164 KB. We need to fit tiles for Q, K, V into SMEM. If `D=128`, a tile of `Br x D` for Q, and `Bc x D` for K and V. If `Br=64, Bc=64`, then: - Q tile: `64 * 128 * 4 = 32 KB` - K tile: `64 * 128 * 4 = 32 KB` - V tile: `64 * 128 * 4 = 32 KB` Total ~96 KB. Plus accumulators for S (`64*64*4 = 16 KB`). Total ~112 KB. Fits. If `D=192`: - Q tile: `64 * 192 * 4 = 48 KB` - K tile: `48 KB` - V tile: `48 KB` S tile: `64*64*4 = 16 KB` Total ~160 KB. Still fits in 164 KB but very tight. We might use `Br=32`, `Bc=64` or `Bc=32`. `Br=32, Bc=32, D=192`: - Q tile: `32*192*4 = 24 KB` - K tile: `24 KB` - V tile: `24 KB` - S tile: `32*32*4 = 4 KB` Total ~76 KB. Very comfortable. Because seq_len=512 is small and fixed, we can even write a kernel where each thread block computes one (batch, head) and loops over the entire KV sequence. For example: - Block computes one (batch, head, query_token). Since S=512, if we have 256 threads per block, each thread computes one query row. - Each query row loads its Q vector (1xD) from global memory. - Then loops over KV tiles: load K tile to SMEM, compute dot product, accumulate scores, do online softmax. - After all scores computed, load V tiles to SMEM and compute weighted sum. This is similar to FlashAttention but simplified because each thread handles one query token. 512 threads per (batch, head) is not enough (512 blocks). But if we process multiple query tokens per block, we get more parallelism. Simpler approach: Since S=512 is small, we can load the *entire* K and V for a KV head into shared memory for each block. Block per (batch, head, query_tile). For a KV head, K and V shape is `[S, D]`. If `S=512, D=128`, size is `512*128*4 = 256 KB`. This *exceeds* shared memory per SM (164 KB) on A100. So we cannot load the full K/V sequence into SMEM if D=128. But if D=192, `512*192*4 = 384 KB`. Definitely too big. So we must tile over the sequence dimension as well. However, we can keep K and V in registers if we process one KV at a time. Another approach: One warp per query token. - Launch `B * H * S` warps? That's too many threads if each warp has 32 threads. `4*16*512 = 32768` warps -> 1M threads. That might be okay if occupancy allows, but launching 1M threads could be heavy. Actually, we can have one block per (batch, head), and inside the block, one warp per query token. 512 warps per block is too many (512 * 32 = 16384 threads per block). Max threads per block is 1024. So max 32 warps. So one block handles `1024/32 = 32` query tokens. 512 queries / 32 = 16 blocks per head. Total blocks = `B * H * 16`. With `B=4, H=16`, total = 1024 blocks. This is decent. Let's design: - BlockDim: `(32, 32, 1)` -> 1024 threads. `threadIdx.x` is lane within warp. `threadIdx.y` is warp id. - Each warp handles one query token. `threadIdx.y` in `[0, 31]` -> 32 query tokens per block. - GridDim: `(num_blocks_per_head, H, B)` where `num_blocks_per_head = ceil(S / 32) = 16`. - Block `(bx, by, bz)` handles batch `bz`, head `by`, query tokens from `q_start = bx * 32` to `q_end = min((bx+1)*32, S)`. So `threadIdx.y` selects the query token within the tile: `q = q_start + threadIdx.y`. Each warp computes one output vector of size D. Algorithm for one warp (query token `q`): 1. Load Q vector for token `q` into registers (or shared memory per warp). 2. Initialize `m = -inf`, `l = 0`, accumulator `o = 0` (length D). 3. Loop over KV sequence in chunks of `Bc` (e.g., `Bc=32`): a. Load K tile `[Bc, D]` to shared memory. Each thread in the block loads some elements. b. Compute `s[k] = dot(Q, K[k]) / sqrt(D)` for `k=0..Bc-1`. - Each warp computes its own scores. We can have warp-level reduction or have threads compute partial sums. - Since `threadIdx.x` are 32 threads, and D might be 128 or 192, split D across threads. Each thread computes partial dot product of Q and K[k] for a slice of D. - Then `__shfl_sync` or warp shuffle to sum up. - Save scores in registers: `s[bc]` for k in `0..Bc-1`. Since `Bc=32`, each warp can keep 32 floats in registers. c. Apply causal mask: if `q < kv_idx`, `s[k] = -inf`. d. Online softmax update: compute `m_new = max(m, max_k(s[k]))`, `p[k] = exp(s[k] - m_new)`, `l_new = l * exp(m - m_new) + sum_k(p[k])`, `o_new = o * exp(m - m_new) + sum_k(p[k] * V[k])`. - `exp(m - m_new)` is a scalar. - We need `p[k]` and we need to multiply by `V[k]` and sum. - This requires loading V tile. e. Load V tile `[Bc, D]` to shared memory (or reuse K SMEM after loading V). f. For each `d` in D (split across threads): - Compute sum over k: `sum = 0`. For `k` in `0..Bc-1`: `sum += p[k] * V[k][d]`. - Update `o[d] = o[d] * exp(m - m_new) + sum`. g. Update `m = m_new`, `l = l_new`. 4. After loop, normalize: `o[d] /= l`. 5. Write `o` to global memory `[B, H, S, D]` at position `(bz, by, q, d)`. This is a clean kernel, similar to FlashAttention but simplified with one warp per query. Memory: - K tile and V tile: `Bc * D * 4 bytes`. With `Bc=32`, `D=192`: `32*192*4 = 24 KB`. We can double-buffer or reuse the same SMEM. Total SMEM per block ~24 KB + maybe Q vector per warp? We don't need Q in SMEM; each warp loads its Q into registers distributed among threads. Let's estimate registers. Each thread needs: - partial Q slice (e.g., D/32 values): `192/32 = 6` floats -> 6 registers. - partial O accumulator: 6 floats -> 6 registers. - scores `p[k]`: 32 floats -> 32 registers. - `m`, `l`, `exp(m-m_new)`: a few registers. Total per thread: ~50 registers. * 32 threads/warp = 1600 per warp. * 32 warps = 51200 registers per block. Max per block is 65536. Fits. This kernel should be quite efficient. It avoids writing intermediate attention scores to global memory. We need to handle GQA. K and V have shape `[B, Hkv, S, D]`. When a block processes head `by`, the KV head index is `by / (H / Hkv)`. So in the kernel, we compute `kv_head = head / num_queries_per_kv`. We need `num_heads` and `num_kv_heads` passed as arguments. Let's assume `num_heads` and `num_kv_heads` are known at runtime. We can get them from the weight shapes in `__init__`. We need to write the CUDA kernel and a C++ wrapper to bind it to PyTorch. Also, we need to replace the attention forward. We can keep the QKV and O projections as PyTorch linear layers (which do their GEMM via cuBLAS). Then after QKV proj, reshape and transpose. Then call our custom kernel. Then reshape and call O proj. This means we still have QKV/O GEMMs but we fused the attention math. Can we fuse QKV projection into the kernel? We could, but it requires loading weight matrices into SMEM and doing GEMM within the kernel. That is much harder and cuBLAS is highly optimized for these GEMMs. The tensor cores would be underutilized in our hand-written CUDA GEMM. So it's probably best to keep QKV and O as cuBLAS GEMMs and just fuse the attention math. Wait, but if the reference uses SDPA, then the QKV/O GEMMs are still there, and the attention core is already fused. We'd be fusing something that's already fused. In that case, our custom kernel for the core might not be faster than SDPA, and we'd still have QKV/O overheads. So the total time would be similar or slower. How to gain speedup over the SDPA reference? If SDPA is used, the main inefficiency is not in the attention core but in the multiple kernel launches around it: reshape, transpose, memory copies for `causal_mask`, etc. But `transformers` SDPA also does these. Actually, `transformers` implementation of SDPA typically does: ```python query_states = self.q_proj(hidden_states).view(...).transpose(1,2) key_states = self.k_proj(hidden_states).view(...).transpose(1,2) value_states = self.v_proj(hidden_states).view(...).transpose(1,2) attn_output = torch.nn.functional.scaled_dot_product_attention(...) attn_output = attn_output.transpose(1,2).contiguous().view(...) attn_output = self.o_proj(attn_output) ``` The SDPA kernel itself is very fast. But the `transpose(1,2)` and `.contiguous()` calls around it cause memory copies. Our custom kernel can operate on `B, S, H, D` layout directly, or call `contiguous()` once. But we might not beat the overall layer time if SDPA is already fast. Let's consider a different angle: What if we replace the MLP layer with a fused custom kernel? The MLP in transformers is: ```python gate = self.gate_proj(x) up = self.up_proj(x) x = self.down_proj(self.act_fn(gate) * up) ``` Or for older style: ```python x = self.c_fc(x) x = self.act(x) x = self.c_proj(x) ``` Even if cuBLAS handles each GEMM efficiently, the activation and multiplication are memory-bound kernels that read/write the large `[B*S, intermediate_size]` tensor. Fusing `act(gate) * up -> down` into a single kernel could save significant memory bandwidth. Specifically, `gate`, `up`, and `act` elements are consumed once. If we fuse `down_proj` with the elementwise ops, we don't need to write `gate`, `up`, or `act(gate)*up` to global memory. We just read `x`, compute `gate` and `up` slices on the fly, multiply, and accumulate into the output of `down_proj`. Wait, but `down_proj` is a GEMM: `[B*S, inter] @ [inter, hidden]`. To fuse the elementwise ops with the GEMM, we'd need to perform the GEMM while generating the activation on the fly. This is possible if we write a custom GEMM kernel where the A matrix is computed on-the-fly. For example, for each output tile `[M_tile, N_tile]`, we accumulate over K dimension (`intermediate_size`). For each K tile, we load the M_tile x K_tile slice of `x`, then compute the corresponding slices of `gate` and `up` using the weight tiles, apply activation and multiplication, then multiply with the `down_proj` weight tile and accumulate. This is extremely complex. Another alternative kernel: Since `sequence_length=512` is fixed and relatively small, we could do the entire text generation forward pass layer-by-layer but keep weights on GPU and activations in registers/SMEM. This is too complex. What if the reference implementation does *not* use SDPA? The `transformers` version in the environment might be old. Let's check typical environment for these tasks. It's often a recent environment: Python 3.10, PyTorch 2.1+, transformers 4.36+. In recent `transformers`, the default `attn_implementation` is `eager`. Actually, I think `transformers` defaults to `eager` unless `sdpa` is explicitly requested or the model supports it and `torch>=2.0`. Wait, in `transformers`, `AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(...)` with no explicit `attn_implementation`: as of `transformers>=4.36`, it defaults to checking if the model supports SDPA and uses it. No, actually the default is `None`, which means it tries to use the most optimized one available: Flash Attention 2 if installed, else SDPA if supported and `torch>=2.0`, else `eager`. Let me check: For `LlamaModel`, `from_pretrained` with `torch_dtype=torch.float16` and `device_map="auto"` might use `sdpa`. But for fp32, SDPA is still called if available. The `scaled_dot_product_attention` works in fp32 but is less optimized than FlashAttention (which is usually fp16). The memory-efficient attention backend supports fp32 though. If `attn_implementation` is `eager`, then the attention is definitely our best target. If `attn_implementation` is `sdpa`, then the core attention is already fused. Our fused attention kernel might be competitive but not necessarily faster than the highly tuned `torch.nn.functional.scaled_dot_product_attention` (which calls cuDNN or xFormers-like kernels). However, the surrounding QKV/O projections and transposes are still there. A better target if SDPA is used: the MLP `norm -> gate_proj -> up_proj -> activation -> down_proj`. The elementwise ops (`act`, `mul`) between the GEMMs cause memory round-trips. We can write a custom kernel that fuses `gate_proj`, `up_proj`, `act`, `mul`, and `down_proj`? No, as discussed, fusing GEMMs is hard. But we can write a kernel for `act(gate) * up` followed by `down_proj`? No, `down_proj` is GEMM. What about fusing the MLP's two matmuls by calling `torch.matmul` with a custom `output`? There's `torch.addmm`, but no fusion. How about we write a custom kernel for the entire *decoder layer* by calling `torch.matmul` for the GEMMs inside the kernel? That's impossible from CUDA. Wait, we can write an `torch.autograd.Function` that uses `torch` operations for the GEMMs (which call cuBLAS) and custom CUDA kernels for the elementwise parts. For example, after QKV projection, instead of doing explicit transposes and views (which are memory-bound and create non-contiguous tensors), we can write a custom kernel that reads Q, K, V in their original layout (e.g., `[B, S, H, D]`) and computes attention. This avoids the transpose overhead. Similarly, after attention, the reference does `transpose(1,2).contiguous().view(B, S, H*D)`. We can avoid this by having our kernel write output in `[B, S, H, D]` and then a custom reshape kernel, or just `reshape` (which is free). Then `o_proj` takes `[B, S, H*D]`. If the reference uses `eager`, then the core attention is `Q @ K^T -> scale -> mask -> softmax -> dropout -> @V`. This is 4-5 kernel launches plus materializing the large `[B,H,S,S]` attention scores. For B=4, H=16, S=512: `4*16*512*512 = 16,777,216` floats. That's 64MB of memory read/written just for attention scores. Our kernel avoids this entirely. This is a huge win and almost certainly faster than the eager reference. If SDPA is used, the memory traffic for attention scores is avoided. But do we still win? The SDPA kernel is very optimized. But we can maybe match it and also avoid the `transpose(1,2)` overheads. If `head_dim=128`, and `S=512`, we can write an extremely efficient kernel. Let's design a kernel for `[B, H, S, D]` layout. We can do one block per (batch, head, query_tile). Query tile size `Br=32` or `64`. `Bc=64` for KV tile. `D=128`. SMEM usage: - Q tile: `Br * D * 4` = `32*128*4 = 16KB` (if Br=32) or `64*128*4 = 32KB` (if Br=64). - K tile: `Bc * D * 4` = `64*128*4 = 32KB`. - V tile: `32KB`. Total: 80KB per block. Fits. If `Br=64`, total = 96KB. Fits. But `num_attention_heads=24`, `num_kv_heads=4`. So for query heads, the KV heads are repeated. A block for head `h` should load KV head `h / (H / Hkv)`. `H / Hkv = 24 / 4 = 6`. So 6 query heads share 1 KV head. Let's design: - Grid: `(div_up(S, Br), H, B)` Each block computes output for one (batch, head), for query positions in `[q_start, q_start+Br)`. Within a block: - Threads: `(D, Br/??)`? Let's use 2D threads. For `D=128`, if we use `threadIdx.x = 128`, then `threadIdx.y = Br`. `Br` cannot be too large. `128 * Br` threads. Max 1024, so `Br <= 8`. That's too small. Alternative: - `threadIdx.x = 32` (warp size), `threadIdx.y = num_warps`. Total threads = `32 * num_warps`. - We can have `num_warps = Br`. So `Br` warps of 32 threads. If `Br=32`, total threads = 1024. Perfect. - Each warp handles one query token. - `threadIdx.y` identifies the query token within the tile. - `threadIdx.x` (0..31) helps load parts of K/V tiles and compute partial dot products. For `D=128`, and 32 threads in a warp: each thread handles `128/32 = 4` dimensions. So each thread loads 4 floats of Q for its assigned query token. Registers for Q: 4 floats. Accumulator O: 4 floats. Scores: we need to compute `s[k]` for `k=0..Bc-1`. Bc=64. But a warp computes 64 dot products of `Q` (128d) with `K[k]` (128d). Approach: Load K tile `[Bc, D]` (e.g., 64x128) into SMEM. Each warp computes scores for its query `q` against all 64 KV tokens. Since each thread has a slice of Q (4 floats), it can load the corresponding slice of each K row from SMEM. For each `k` from `0` to `Bc-1`: - thread loads `k_slice = K[k, threadIdx.x*4 : threadIdx.x*4 + 4]` from SMEM. - computes `dot_partial = sum(Q_slice * k_slice)`. - Then warp-reduce `dot_partial` across 32 threads using `__shfl_xor_sync`. - Result is the full dot product `s[k]`. - This requires `Bc` iterations per warp, and each iteration does a small amount of compute. For `Bc=64`, total partial loads per thread: `64 * 4 = 256` floats. Total compute: 256 FMAs. Very small. Then we need to load V tile and compute weighted sum. Load V tile `[Bc, D]` to SMEM. For each output dimension `d` (4 per thread): - sum over `k`: `p[k] * V[k, d]`. This can be done by iterating `k=0..Bc-1`, reading `V[k, d]` and `p[k]`. `p[k]` is stored in registers (64 floats per warp). Each thread loads one `V[k,d]` per `k`. There are `Bc=64` loads and `Bc` accumulations for each of the 4 output dimensions. Total loads: `64*4 = 256`. So total register/SMEM usage is fine. Time for one warp per query token, block of 32 warps: Per block: - Q load: `32*128*4 = 16KB` from global mem. Loading partially per thread. - K/V tile loads: for each of `S/Bc = 512/64 = 8` tiles: - Load K tile: `64*128*4 = 32KB` - Load V tile: `32KB` Total HBM read per block: `16KB + 8 * 64KB = 528KB`. Write: `32*128*4 = 16KB`. Total blocks: `B * H * (S/Br) = 4 * 24 * 16 = 1536`. Total HBM traffic for attention core: `1536 * 544KB ≈ 816 MB`. For the reference eager implementation: Q: `B*H*S*D * 4 = 4*24*512*128*4 = 25MB` K: `B*Hkv*S*D*4 = 4*4*512*128*4 = 4.2MB` V: same 4.2MB Q@K^T output: `B*H*S*S*4 = 4*24*512*512*4 = 100.6MB` Softmax output (inplace or not): another 100MB. Attn@V output: `B*H*S*D*4 = 25MB` So total reads/writes: Q (25), K (4), V (4), Q@K^T (read Q, read K, write 100), softmax (read 100, write 100), attn@V (read 100, read V, write 25). Arithmetic reads/writes in FLOPs-limited sense: at least ~350MB, plus intermediate storage operations. But more importantly, our kernel fuses everything, avoiding the large `[S,S]` tensor entirely. The memory bandwidth of 816MB is modest. At 1.5 TB/s, this is <1ms. The compute is also modest. Actually, the main cost of the reference eager implementation is not memory bandwidth but the launch of many kernels and reading/writing the 100MB attention matrix multiple times. So our kernel will be much faster. Let's also consider QKV projections. These are 3 GEMMs. `Q: [2048, 3072] @ [3072, 3072] -> 2048*3072*3072*2 = 38.7 GFLOPs` `K: [2048, 3072] @ [3072, 512] -> 6.4 GFLOPs` (since Hkv*D = 4*128=512) `V: same 6.4 GFLOPs` `O: [2048, 3072] @ [3072, 3072] -> 38.7 GFLOPs` Total attention GEMMs: ~90 GFLOPs. The core attention for S=512, D=128: QK: `B*H*S*S*D*2 = 4*24*512*512*128*2 = 5.1 GFLOPs`? Wait. Matmul of `[S,D]` and `[D,S]` for each head: `2*S*S*D`. Total FLOPs = `B*H*2*S*S*D = 4*24*2*512*512*128 = 6.44 GFLOPs`. Attn@V: `B*H*2*S*S*D = same 6.44 GFLOPs`. Total core attention FLOPs: ~13 GFLOPs. So the core attention is actually FLOP-light. It is memory-bound due to the `[S,S]` matrix. Our kernel reduces memory traffic, but the GEMMs dominate the time. However, avoiding the `[S,S]` tensor and kernel launch overhead should still be significant. For the overall model runtime, MLP dominates. Each MLP (if SwiGLU): `gate_proj`: `[2048, 3072] @ [3072, 12288] = 154 GFLOPs` `up_proj`: same `154 GFLOPs` `down_proj`: `[2048, 12288] @ [12288, 3072] = 154 GFLOPs` Total MLP per layer: ~462 GFLOPs. For 24 layers: ~11 TFLOPs. Attention GEMMs per layer: 90 GFLOPs. For 24 layers: ~2.1 TFLOPs. Total: ~13 TFLOPs of GEMMs. So MLP is ~5x the FLOPs of attention. Replacing just the attention core saves maybe 10-20% of total time? The reference eager might spend a lot of time on attention because `[S,S]` is large. Wait, S=512. `[S,S]` is 512*512 = 262K elements per head. `B*H = 96`. Total elements = 25M. That's not huge. In fp32 it's 100MB. For A100 memory bandwidth ~1.5 TB/s, reading/writing 100MB takes ~0.13ms. 4 layers of this (read, write, read, write) = 0.5ms per layer. 24 layers = 12ms. The MLP GEMMs at 462 GFLOPs per layer. At 100 TFLOPS, that's 4.6ms per layer. 24 layers = 110ms. So MLP is the bulk. Attention core via eager is maybe 10-20ms total. So replacing attention core might only save ~10-20ms out of ~120ms. A 10% speedup might be enough if the baseline is not fully optimized. But if the baseline uses SDPA, the attention core is already fast (0.2ms per layer?), making our improvement negligible. So we need a different target to get a robust speedup. The MLP is the dominant cost. How can we accelerate the MLP with a custom CUDA kernel without rewriting a full GEMM? Idea: Replace `nn.Linear` with a custom kernel? No, because `nn.Linear` uses cuBLAS, which is very fast. Idea: Fuse `gate_proj`, `up_proj`, `act`, `mul`, `down_proj`. This avoids writing `gate` and `up` to memory, and reading them back. Can we do this without writing a GEMM kernel? Yes! We can compute `gate_proj` and `up_proj` with standard `torch.matmul` (or `nn.Linear`), but instead of writing the full `activation` tensor to memory, we can compute it in tiles inside a custom kernel that consumes the `gate` and `up` tiles and computes `down_proj` on the fly? But `down_proj` is a GEMM. We can't call cuBLAS from inside our custom CUDA kernel. We'd have to implement the GEMM ourselves. That is hard. However, note that `sequence_length=512` and `batch_size=4`. The MLP matrices are large, but the batch dimension `M=2048` is modest. Could we transpose the computation to do `down_proj` as a batched matmul where the "batch" is the sequence length? No. Alternative idea: Use `torch.compile` on the MLP block? The task says "custom CUDA kernel". But perhaps we can write a custom kernel for the elementwise ops *between* the GEMMs, which still reduces memory traffic. For SwiGLU: ``` gate = gate_proj(x) # [2048, 12288] up = up_proj(x) # [2048, 12288] act = silu(gate) * up # elementwise y = down_proj(act) # [2048, 3072] ``` The elementwise `silu(gate) * up` reads `gate` and `up` (2 reads) and writes `act` (1 write). Total 3 * 2048 * 12288 * 4 bytes = ~300MB. Custom kernel can fuse `silu(gate) * up -> act`. That's 1 read of gate, 1 read of up, 1 write of act. Same traffic as `torch` implementation? Actually, PyTorch `torch.nn.functional.silu(gate) * up` launches two kernels: `silu` (read gate, write tmp) and `mul` (read tmp, read up, write act). So it reads gate twice and writes/reads a tmp. Total traffic: 5 * size. Our fused kernel does 3 * size. So we save 40% of memory traffic for that step. On memory-bound ops, this is a win. Then `act` is consumed by `down_proj`. So we save the intermediate tmp. Similarly, after `down_proj`, there is a residual add. And before MLP, there is LayerNorm. We can fuse LayerNorm + residual? Or LayerNorm + gate_proj/up_proj? No. We can write a custom kernel for `silu(gate) * up`. But is that enough for a speedup? For 24 layers, 300MB saved per layer = 7.2GB. At 1.5TB/s, this is ~4.8ms. That's minor compared to 110ms of compute. What about the entire MLP? What if we replace the `Starcoder2MLP` module with a custom kernel that calls `torch.matmul` for gate and up, then our fused activation, then `torch.matmul` for down. We only save the activation elementwise overhead. Still small. What if we write a custom kernel that does the entire MLP using CUTLASS-like kernels? Too hard. Let's think about what operations are memory-bound and numerous. Transformer layer (StarCoder2): 1. Input LayerNorm 2. QKV proj (GEMMs) 3. Attention core 4. O proj (GEMM) 5. Residual add 6. Post-Attention LayerNorm 7. Gate proj (GEMM) 8. Up proj (GEMM) 9. Activation + Mul 10. Down proj (GEMM) 11. Residual add Kernels 1, 5, 6, 9, 11 are memory-bound elementwise kernels. Kernels 2, 4, 7, 8, 10 are compute-bound GEMMs. If we could fuse the memory-bound kernels with the compute-bound ones, we'd save time. For example, fusing residual add before MLP with LayerNorm? That's a standard fusion. But we can just write a fused `add + layernorm` kernel. Similarly, fuse `add` after O-proj and after Down-proj. But `transformers` usually does these as separate `nn.LayerNorm`. Actually, a very common optimization in these kernel tasks is noticing that the reference does *not* use `torch.nn.functional.scaled_dot_product_attention` and writing a FlashAttention kernel gives a massive speedup, often 2x or more. Even if we only save 10-20ms out of 120ms, if the reference runtime is, say, 200ms, and we save 50ms (if reference is memory bound or has high overhead), we get a good speedup. Also, if the baseline is not using SDPA, attention core might be 30-40% of the time for S=512 if the GEMMs are not fully saturating the GPU (e.g., on smaller GPUs or because of kernel launch overhead). But let's think about the actual evaluation. It's a competition. People will write a custom kernel. What gives the best speedup? If the baseline is not compiled with `torch.compile`, then the attention is eager. A fused attention kernel is a classic move and gives huge speedup. If the baseline uses SDPA, then the attention is already fused. What can we target? Maybe we can replace the entire `Starcoder2DecoderLayer` with a custom kernel that calls cuBLAS for GEMMs but fuses elementwise ops? We cannot call cuBLAS from a CUDA kernel easily. Wait, can we use `torch` inside our custom kernel? No. The CUDA kernel is pure CUDA. But `ModelNew.forward` is Python. We could reimplement the whole forward in Python, calling our custom kernels for parts and `torch.matmul` for others. For example: ```python def custom_mlp(x, fc1_weight, fc2_weight): # ... do gate_proj and up_proj with torch.matmul gate = F.linear(x, fc1_weight) up = F.linear(x, up_weight) # call custom kernel for silu(gate)*up act = silu_mul_kernel(gate, up) # down proj return F.linear(act, down_weight) ``` This replaces 2-3 PyTorch elementwise kernels with 1 custom kernel. Modest gain. Alternatively, we can write a custom kernel for the *entire* MLP by decomposing the GEMMs into blocks and performing them with tensor cores? This is well beyond the scope. If the reference attention is eager, the forward pass of one attention block does: - 4 `nn.Linear` calls (Q, K, V, O). Each linear is a GEMM + add bias. - Reshape/permute (cheap meta ops). - `matmul` Q@K.T (kernel 1). - `/sqrt(...)` (kernel 2). - `+ mask` (kernel 3). - `softmax` (kernel 4). - `matmul` Attn@V (kernel 5). - `transpose` + `reshape` + `contiguous` (potentially kernel 6). - `nn.Linear` O. This is ~10 kernel launches per layer, plus the large `S*S` intermediate. Our kernel replaces kernels 1-5 and the transpose/reshape into just one kernel launch. Even if SDPA is used, our kernel might beat it for fp32 on small seq len because FlashAttention-2 in fp32 is less optimized or falls back to memory-efficient attention. The memory-efficient attention in PyTorch (for fp32) does block-wise softmax and keeps tiles in memory, but still may require multiple SRAM-level tiles. It is likely quite fast. Another crucial point: The task says "produce numerically equivalent outputs at fp32 precision." The `torch.nn.functional.scaled_dot_product_attention` dispatch: - If all conditions met for FlashAttention, use it (requires fp16/bf16, sm80+, head_dim <= 128, etc.). - Else if conditions met for Memory-Efficient Attention, use it (supports fp32, various head_dims, but seq_len must be > 0). What else can we write a kernel for that is easier and gives decent speedup? - `LayerNorm`? It's memory-bandwidth bound. Fusing it with preceding or following ops saves memory. But `transformers` `LayerNorm` is already fairly efficient. - `Rotary Embedding`? It's small. - `Gated MLP activation` (`silu(gate) * up`)? Fusing this helps a bit. What if we write a custom kernel that fuses `silu(gate) * up` AND `down_proj` AND the following `add` AND `layer_norm`? We can't fuse `down_proj` without a GEMM. Wait, `down_proj` is `y = act @ W_down.T + b_down`. We could write a custom kernel that computes this as: For each output element `y[i, j]`: `y[i,j] = sum_k act[i,k] * W_down[k,j] + b_down[j]` And `act[i,k] = silu(gate[i,k]) * up[i,k]`. So `act[i,k]` is computed on-the-fly. This means: `y[i,j] = sum_k (silu(gate[i,k]) * up[i,k]) * W_down[k,j] + b_down[j]`. If we have `gate` and `up` already computed (2 GEMMs), then for the `down_proj` GEMM, instead of loading `act` from global memory, we load `gate` and `up` and compute `act` inside the kernel. This halves the memory bandwidth for loading the activation matrix for `down_proj`. Normally `down_proj` loads `act` (one read). Our fused kernel loads `gate` and `up` (two reads). That's worse! But `gate` and `up` are already in memory. `act` is also in memory. So total traffic reference: read gate, write act_tmp? Wait, reference: `gate = x @ W_g` (write gate), `up = x @ W_u` (write up), `act = silu(gate)*up` (read gate, read up, write act), `y = act @ W_d` (read act). Total reads for activations: gate (once, by act kernel), up (once, by act kernel), act (once, by down kernel). Our fused kernel for down_proj: reads gate, reads up (instead of act). Total 2 reads vs 1 read. So it's worse unless we avoid writing `act` to HBM, but `gate` and `up` are already written to HBM. So we don't save much. Unless we compute `gate` and `up` inside the kernel too! If we compute `gate` and `up` on the fly from `x` and their weights, then `down_proj` loads `x` once, and computes partial products of `gate` and `up` in SMEM/registers, then `silu` and `mul`, then accumulates into `y`. This requires loading weight tiles for `W_g`, `W_u`, and `W_d`. But `W_g` and `W_u` are large (`H x I` each). Loading them alongside `W_d` (I x H) is a lot. But the total FLOPs is still the same. Memory for weights is 3 matrices vs 1 matrix (W_d) + 2 activations (gate, up). Let's compare: Reference: - Read x (M*H) for gate and up GEMMs. In practice, cuBLAS reads x twice (or cached in L2). - Weights W_g, W_u read once each. - Write gate (M*I), write up (M*I). - Read gate (M*I), read up (M*I) for act. Write act (M*I). - Read act (M*I), weights W_d (I*H) for down. Write y (M*H). Total activation traffic: M*I * (2 write gate/up + 2 read gate/up + 1 write act + 1 read act) = 6 M*I. Fused from x: - Read x (M*H) once. - Read W_g, W_u, W_d once each. - No intermediate activation traffic. But we need to perform 3 GEMMs. Fusing them means we need to load x, and for each tile of output, load slices of W_g and W_u to compute a tile of gate/up in registers, then load W_d tile to compute output. This is exactly the MLP fusion used in optimized inference engines. This is complex but doable using CUTLASS? But we have to write raw CUDA. However, maybe we can use `torch`'s `addmm` and a custom kernel for the activation only. Given the constraints (8 turns, need to write and debug CUDA), targeting the attention core is the most straightforward way to write a correct and fast kernel. It's a self-contained algorithm that avoids GEMM complexity. And for 512 seq length, a simple warp-based kernel is quite powerful. Even if SDPA is present, we might win by avoiding layout transformations. Also, note that the StarCoder2 model has `num_attention_heads=24`, `num_key_value_heads=4`. The reference `transformers` attention with GQA might have some inefficiency in broadcasting K/V to all query heads. Our kernel handles GQA directly. Wait, there's another part: the `o_proj` bias? What if we write a kernel for the **entire attention block**: QKV bias addition + RoPE + attention + O proj + residual add? That's way too much. Let's examine the exact `transformers` code for `Starcoder2Attention`: ```python class Starcoder2Attention(nn.Module): def __init__(self, config: Starcoder2Config, layer_idx: Optional[int] = None): super().__init__() ... self.q_proj = nn.Linear(config.hidden_size, self.num_heads * self.head_dim, bias=True) self.k_proj = nn.Linear(config.hidden_size, self.num_key_value_heads * self.head_dim, bias=True) self.v_proj = nn.Linear(config.hidden_size, self.num_key_value_heads * self.head_dim, bias=True) self.o_proj = nn.Linear(self.num_heads * self.head_dim, config.hidden_size, bias=config.attention_bias) self.rotary_emb = Starcoder2RotaryEmbedding(config=self.config) def forward(self, hidden_states, attention_mask, position_ids, past_key_value, output_attentions, use_cache, cache_position): bsz, q_len, _ = hidden_states.size() query_states = self.q_proj(hidden_states) key_states = self.k_proj(hidden_states) value_states = self.v_proj(hidden_states) query_states = query_states.view(bsz, q_len, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2) key_states = key_states.view(bsz, q_len, self.num_key_value_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2) value_states = value_states.view(bsz, q_len, self.num_key_value_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2) cos, sin = self.rotary_emb(value_states, seq_len=kv_seq_len) query_states, key_states = apply_rotary_pos_emb(query_states, key_states, cos, sin, unsqueeze_dim=1) attn_output = torch.nn.functional.scaled_dot_product_attention(...) attn_output = attn_output.transpose(1, 2).contiguous() attn_output = attn_output.view(bsz, q_len, self.hidden_size) attn_output = self.o_proj(attn_output) return attn_output, ... ``` So there are `view` and `transpose` ops, plus `rotary_emb`. The `rotary_emb` is applied after projection. Even if SDPA is used, the `transpose(1,2)` on input and output causes strides. Monkey-patching: In `ModelNew.__init__`, after loading the model, iterate over `model.model.layers` (or `.transformer.h` depending on exact model class name). Starcoder2 uses `model.model.layers` (the internal model is `Starcoder2Model`, which has `layers`). For each layer, replace `layer.self_attn.forward` with our custom wrapper. Our custom wrapper `custom_attn_forward(self, hidden_states, attention_mask=None, position_ids=None, past_key_value=None, output_attentions=False, use_cache=False, cache_position=None)`: 1. `bsz, q_len, _ = hidden_states.size()` 2. Compute Q, K, V with `self.q_proj`, `self.k_proj`, `self.v_proj`. These return `[B, S, H*D]`. 3. Apply rotary embeddings if needed. We can call `self.rotary_emb` and `apply_rotary_pos_emb` as in the original. This is fine because it's cheap. 4. Reshape to `[B, H, S, D]`. 5. So our wrapper: ```python def custom_attn_forward(self, hidden_states, attention_mask=None, position_ids=None, past_key_value=None, output_attentions=False, use_cache=False, cache_position=None, **kwargs): bsz, q_len, _ = hidden_states.shape query_states = self.q_proj(hidden_states) key_states = self.k_proj(hidden_states) value_states = self.v_proj(hidden_states) # rotary kv_seq_len = key_states.shape[-2] query_states = query_states.view(bsz, q_len, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1,2) key_states = key_states.view(bsz, q_len, self.num_key_value_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1,2) value_states = value_states.view(bsz, q_len, self.num_key_value_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1,2) cos, sin = self.rotary_emb(value_states, seq_len=kv_seq_len) query_states, key_states = apply_rotary_pos_emb(query_states, key_states, cos, sin) # custom kernel expects contiguous [B, H, S, D] query_states = query_states.contiguous() key_states = key_states.contiguous() value_states = value_states.contiguous() attn_output = custom_attention_forward(query_states, key_states, value_states) attn_output = attn_output.transpose(1,2).contiguous().view(bsz, q_len, self.hidden_size) attn_output = self.o_proj(attn_output) return attn_output, None, past_key_value ``` Wait, `rotary_emb` takes `position_ids` and `seq_len`. We can simply drop it and apply causal logic in the kernel. What about `sliding_window` or other masks? Starcoder2 likely uses full causal. So it's fine. Now, `rotary_emb`: In the `transformers` implementation, `apply_rotary_pos_emb` is called on Q and K. We can keep this as is in our wrapper. Writing the monkey patch: ```python import transformers.models.starcoder2.modeling_starcoder2 as star_module original_attn_forward = star_module.Starcoder2Attention.forward def patched_attn_forward(self, hidden_states, attention_mask=None, position_ids=None, past_key_value=None, output_attentions=False, use_cache=False, cache_position=None, **kwargs): bsz, q_len, _ = hidden_states.size() query_states = self.q_proj(hidden_states) key_states = self.k_proj(hidden_states) value_states = self.v_proj(hidden_states) query_states = query_states.view(bsz, q_len, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2) key_states = key_states.view(bsz, q_len, self.num_key_value_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2) value_states = value_states.view(bsz, q_len, self.num_key_value_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2) kv_seq_len = key_states.shape[-2] cos, sin = self.rotary_emb(value_states, seq_len=kv_seq_len) query_states, key_states = apply_rotary_pos_emb(query_states, key_states, cos, sin) # Ensure contiguous for our kernel query_states = query_states.contiguous() key_states = key_states.contiguous() value_states = value_states.contiguous() attn_output = custom_attention_forward(query_states, key_states, value_states) attn_output = attn_output.transpose(1, 2).contiguous() attn_output = attn_output.view(bsz, q_len, self.hidden_size) attn_output = self.o_proj(attn_output) return attn_output, None, past_key_value # Patch all layers for layer in self.model.model.layers: layer.self_attn.forward = types.MethodType(patched_attn_forward, layer.self_attn) ``` We need to import `apply_rotary_pos_emb` from the modeling module. But `rotary_emb` might require `position_ids`. The call `self.rotary_emb(value_states, seq_len=kv_seq_len)` is for the case where `position_ids` are not provided, which is an older API. In newer `transformers`, `rotary_emb` might take `position_ids` or `cache_position` and `seq_len`. If we use a fixed version approach, it might break. Since the `model` object already has the original `forward` method which handles this correctly, we could instead intercept the execution of the attention by replacing `torch.matmul` or `F.softmax`? No. Alternative: Instead of monkey-patching `Starcoder2Attention.forward`, we can monkey-patch `torch.nn.functional.scaled_dot_product_attention` AND `torch.matmul` in the specific region. But that's too hacky. Wait, maybe we don't need to monkey-patch. The model class `Starcoder2ForCausalLM` has a `forward` that calls `self.model(...)`. The `self.model` is a `Starcoder2Model`. We could replace `self.model` with a custom `nn.Module` that reimplements the `forward` of `Starcoder2Model`. But reimplementing the whole model is too much. But the `Starcoder2Model` consists of `embed_tokens`, `layers`, and `norm`. We could keep everything but replace each `layer.self_attn` with our own `nn.Module`. ```python class CustomAttention(nn.Module): def __init__(self, original_attn): super().__init__() self.original_attn = original_attn def forward(self, hidden_states, attention_mask=None, position_ids=None, past_key_value=None, output_attentions=False, use_cache=False, cache_position=None, **kwargs): # Compute QKV using original weights query_states = self.original_attn.q_proj(hidden_states) ... # rotary ... attn_output = custom_attention_forward(...) attn_output = self.original_attn.o_proj(attn_output) return attn_output, None, past_key_value ``` Then for each layer: `layer.self_attn = CustomAttention(layer.self_attn)`. This keeps the original weights. The `forward` signature of `Starcoder2DecoderLayer` calls `self.self_attn(hidden_states, ...)` and expects `(attn_output, attn_weights, past_key_value)`. So our `CustomAttention.forward` must match that. This approach avoids copying/patching method code and is robust to `transformers` version changes, because we just wrap the module. The interface between `Starcoder2DecoderLayer` and `Starcoder2Attention` is the `forward` signature. If the signature changes, both the original and our wrapper need to match. But since we wrap the original module and call it as a black box for some parts, we can even do: ```python class CustomAttention(nn.Module): def __init__(self, original): super().__init__() self.q_proj = original.q_proj self.k_proj = original.k_proj self.v_proj = original.v_proj self.o_proj = original.o_proj self.rotary_emb = original.rotary_emb self.num_heads = original.num_heads self.num_key_value_heads = original.num_key_value_heads self.head_dim = original.head_dim self.hidden_size = original.hidden_size def forward(self, hidden_states, attention_mask=None, position_ids=None, past_key_value=None, output_attentions=False, use_cache=False, cache_position=None, **kwargs): # compute Q,K,V, rotary, then our kernel # ... ``` This is robust: we extract attributes we need. What about `apply_rotary_pos_emb`? It's a function in the modeling file. We can import it with a try/except to handle version differences, or we can implement it ourselves! Rotary embedding is simple: For each pair of dimensions `(2i, 2i+1)` and position `pos`: ``` freq = 1.0 / theta^(2i/D) cos = cos(pos * freq) sin = sin(pos * freq) q_rot[2i] = q[2i] * cos - q[2i+1] * sin q_rot[2i+1] = q[2i] * sin + q[2i+1] * cos ``` If we implement our own rotary embedding function in Python using PyTorch ops, it might be slower than the original if it's precomputed. But we can call `self.rotary_emb(...)` from the original module object. How to get `apply_rotary_pos_emb` function? ```python from transformers.models.starcoder2.modeling_starcoder2 import apply_rotary_pos_emb ``` This import should work if the model type is `starcoder2`. Let's write the kernel in CUDA. CUDA Kernel details: We want a kernel `fused_attention_forward` that takes float pointers and dimensions. Template: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cub/cub.cuh> // maybe not needed #include <cmath> #define WARP_SIZE 32 __global__ void fused_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ Q, const float* __restrict__ K, const float* __restrict__ V, float* __restrict__ O, int B, int Hq, int Hkv, int S, int D) { // Block indices int b = blockIdx.z; int h = blockIdx.y; int q_tile = blockIdx.x; int kv_head = h / (Hq / Hkv); // Each block processes Br query tokens const int Br = 32; // warp count = 32 threads per warp? No, threadIdx.y is warp id const int Bc = 64; // Actually, we map threadIdx.y to warp id (0..Br-1) // threadIdx.x to lane id (0..31) // So Br = blockDim.y const int num_warps = blockDim.y; const int tid = threadIdx.x; const int warp_id = threadIdx.y; // Query index int q = q_tile * num_warps + warp_id; if (q >= S) return; // Pointers to Q, K, V, O for this batch and head // Q layout: [B, Hq, S, D] // K layout: [B, Hkv, S, D] const float* q_ptr = Q + ((b * Hq + h) * S + q) * D; const float* k_ptr_base = K + ((b * Hkv + kv_head) * S) * D; const float* v_ptr_base = V + ((b * Hkv + kv_head) * S) * D; float* o_ptr = O + ((b * Hq + h) * S + q) * D; // Load Q into registers (D elements split across 32 threads) float q_reg[16]; // max D/32 = 128/32=4. But allocate enough for D up to e.g., 256/32=8. // We need compile-time or dynamic. Let's do a loop. const int elem_per_thread = (D + WARP_SIZE - 1) / WARP_SIZE; // Actually D is constant to the block? No, runtime. // We can load in a loop. float q_local[8]; // assume max D = 256, so 8 per thread #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < elem_per_thread; ++i) { int d = tid + i * WARP_SIZE; if (d < D) q_local[i] = q_ptr[d]; } // Shared memory for K and V tiles // We'll use dynamic shared memory or fixed maximum size. // Let's use a fixed size assuming max Bc=64, max D=128. // To be generic with D up to say 256, we can allocate per block at launch. // Use `extern __shared__ float smem[];` // K tile and V tile. } ``` The kernel is slightly complex. Let's flesh it out carefully. Dynamic shared memory is easiest. Shared memory layout: - `k_smem[Bc * D]` - `v_smem[Bc * D]` Size in bytes passed at launch: `2 * Bc * D * sizeof(float)`. Algorithm per warp (query token `q`): ``` float m = -INFINITY; float l = 0.0f; float o[elem_per_thread] = {0}; for (int kv_start = 0; kv_start < S; kv_start += Bc) { int actual_Bc = min(Bc, S - kv_start); // Load K tile [actual_Bc, D] to smem // Collaborative load: all threads in block participate // thread linear id = warp_id * WARP_SIZE + tid int t = warp_id * WARP_SIZE + tid; int total_elems = actual_Bc * D; for (int idx = t; idx < total_elems; idx += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { int k_idx = kv_start * D + idx; smem[idx] = k_ptr_base[k_idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute scores s[k] for k=0..actual_Bc-1 // Each warp computes for its query q. float s[32]; // actual_Bc <= Bc. If Bc=64, we need 64 floats per warp. // But registers per thread are limited. Having 64 floats per thread across 32 threads = 64 registers per thread? No, s is per warp, distributed across threads? Not possible to have 64 floats in one thread if Bc=64 and one thread handles all k. // Better: compute s[k] one by one. // For each k in 0..actual_Bc-1: // float dot = 0; // for each i of q_local: // d = tid + i*32 // if (d < D) dot += q_local[i] * smem[k*D + d]; // dot = warp_reduce_sum(dot); // Apply causal mask: if (q < kv_start + k) dot = -INFINITY; // Compute softmax update. // But we need all s[k] to compute max and sum for the tile. // We can process in chunks that fit in registers. } ``` Warp-level reduction: ```cpp __inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); return val; } __inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_max(float val) { for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) val = fmaxf(val, __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset)); return val; } ``` Processing KV tile loop: ```cpp float m = -INFINITY; float l = 0.0f; float o[8] = {0}; // up to D/WARP_SIZE float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)D); for (int kv_start = 0; kv_start < S; kv_start += Bc) { int actual_Bc = min(Bc, S - kv_start); // Load K tile to smem (first half) float* k_smem = smem; // load... __syncthreads(); // First pass: compute tile max float tile_max = -INFINITY; for (int k = 0; k < actual_Bc; ++k) { int kv_pos = kv_start + k; float dot = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < elem_per_thread; ++i) { int d = tid + i * WARP_SIZE; if (d < D) dot += q_local[i] * k_smem[k * D + d]; } dot = warp_reduce_sum(dot) * scale; if (kv_pos > q) dot = -INFINITY; // causal mask (strictly upper triangular; token q attends to tokens <= q) tile_max = fmaxf(tile_max, dot); } // Second pass: compute exp(sum) float tile_sum = 0.0f; // But we need to compute contributions to output in the same loop to save registers? // We need p[k] and need to multiply by V[k,d]. We can compute p[k] on the fly in second pass if we store s[k]. // If actual_Bc=64, storing 64 floats per warp is 64 registers. That's okay. // But having an array `float s_reg[64]` might cause register spilling if not careful. Let's try to process in chunks of e.g., 32. // Let's do two-pass per chunk. // Split actual_Bc into chunks of CHUNK (e.g., 32). #define CHUNK 32 float p_reg[CHUNK]; for (int k_base = 0; k_base < actual_Bc; k_base += CHUNK) { int actual_chunk = min(CHUNK, actual_Bc - k_base); // compute scores for this chunk, store in p_reg float chunk_max = -INFINITY; for (int k = 0; k < actual_chunk; ++k) { int kv_pos = kv_start + k_base + k; float dot = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < elem_per_thread; ++i) { int d = tid + i * WARP_SIZE; if (d < D) dot += q_local[i] * k_smem[(k_base + k) * D + d]; } dot = warp_reduce_sum(dot) * scale; if (kv_pos > q) dot = -INFINITY; p_reg[k] = dot; chunk_max = fmaxf(chunk_max, dot); } // Now we have chunk scores in p_reg. We also need global max. // Online softmax: update m, l, o using this chunk. float m_prev = m; float m_new = fmaxf(m, chunk_max); float exp_scale_prev = expf(m_prev - m_new); l *= exp_scale_prev; // Compute exp(p_reg[k] - m_new) and sum for (int k = 0; k < actual_chunk; ++k) { p_reg[k] = expf(p_reg[k] - m_new); l += p_reg[k]; // wait, warp_reduce_sum needed? No, l is per warp (query), so all threads in warp must sum the same p_reg values. But each thread has the same p_reg[k] after warp_reduce_sum, so we can just sum. // Actually after warp_reduce_sum, all threads in warp have the same dot. So p_reg[k] is identical across threads. // So each thread can compute the same l. No divergence. Fine. } // Update o accumulator: o = o * exp_scale_prev + sum_k p_reg[k] * V[k] // Load V tile chunk to registers or process from smem. // We currently have K in smem. We can reuse the smem for V. // So after K processing, load V tile, then process. } } ``` Wait, this requires reloading V. We can reuse the same shared memory buffer. Let's restructure: ```cpp float m = -INFINITY; float l = 0.0f; float o[elem_per_thread] = {0}; for (int kv_start = 0; kv_start < S; kv_start += Bc) { int actual_Bc = min(Bc, S - kv_start); // Load K tile to smem for (idx...) k_smem[idx] = K[...]; __syncthreads(); // Compute scores for chunk and do online softmax, but we need V too. // Process in sub-chunks of V that fit? No, Bc is small. // Just compute all scores for this tile, store in registers. // actual_Bc <= Bc. Bc can be 32 to limit registers. } ``` To limit registers, set `Bc = 32`. Then `S=512`, so 16 iterations. `CHUNK = 32`. Then `actual_Bc <= 32`. `p_reg[32]` -> 32 floats per thread. If `elem_per_thread = 4` (D=128), total registers ~40. Fine. If D=192, `elem_per_thread = 6`. Total ~40. Still fine. If D=256, `elem_per_thread = 8`. Total ~42. Fine. So set `Bc = 32`. `Br = blockDim.y`. Let's set `blockDim.y = 32` (32 warps). So `Br=32`. Total threads per block = `32 * 32 = 1024`. Blocks per head = `ceil(S / 32) = 16`. SMEM size: `2 * Bc * D * sizeof(float) = 2 * 32 * D * 4`. For D=128: `2*32*128*4 = 32KB`. For D=192: `2*32*192*4 = 48KB`. For D=256: `64KB`. All fine. Kernel launch shared mem: `2 * Bc * D * sizeof(float)`. Let's write the kernel for a generic D. ```cpp __global__ void fused_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ Q, const float* __restrict__ K, const float* __restrict__ V, float* __restrict__ O, int B, int Hq, int Hkv, int S, int D) { const int WARP_SIZE = 32; int b = blockIdx.z; int h = blockIdx.y; int q_tile = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int warp_id = threadIdx.y; int q = q_tile * blockDim.y + warp_id; if (q >= S) return; int kv_head = h / (Hq / Hkv); const float* q_ptr = Q + ((b * Hq + h) * S + q) * D; const float* k_ptr = K + ((b * Hkv + kv_head) * S) * D; const float* v_ptr = V + ((b * Hkv + kv_head) * S) * D; float* o_ptr = O + ((b * Hq + h) * S + q) * D; // Load Q const int elem_per_thread = (D + WARP_SIZE - 1) / WARP_SIZE; float q_reg[8]; // max for D<=256 for (int i = 0; i < elem_per_thread; ++i) { int d = tid + i * WARP_SIZE; if (d < D) q_reg[i] = q_ptr[d]; } extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* k_smem = smem; float* v_smem = smem + 32 * D; // Bc=32 float m = -INFINITY; float l = 0.0f; float o_reg[8] = {0.0f}; const float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)D); float s_reg[32]; // Bc=32 for (int kv_start = 0; kv_start < S; kv_start += 32) { int actual_Bc = min(32, S - kv_start); // Load K tile int total_threads = blockDim.x * blockDim.y; int tidx = warp_id * WARP_SIZE + tid; for (int idx = tidx; idx < actual_Bc * D; idx += total_threads) { k_smem[idx] = k_ptr[(kv_start * D) + idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute scores for (int k = 0; k < actual_Bc; ++k) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < elem_per_thread; ++i) { int d = tid + i * WARP_SIZE; if (d < D) dot += q_reg[i] * k_smem[k * D + d]; } dot = warp_reduce_sum(dot) * scale; int kv_pos = kv_start + k; if (kv_pos > q) dot = -INFINITY; s_reg[k] = dot; } // Softmax update for this tile float tile_max = -INFINITY; for (int k = 0; k < actual_Bc; ++k) tile_max = fmaxf(tile_max, s_reg[k]); float m_new = fmaxf(m, tile_max); float exp_scale = expf(m - m_new); m = m_new; float tile_sum = 0.0f; for (int k = 0; k < actual_Bc; ++k) { s_reg[k] = expf(s_reg[k] - m_new); tile_sum += s_reg[k]; } l = l * exp_scale + tile_sum; // Update o_reg for (int i = 0; i < elem_per_thread; ++i) { o_reg[i] *= exp_scale; } // Load V tile for (int idx = tidx; idx < actual_Bc * D; idx += total_threads) { v_smem[idx] = v_ptr[(kv_start * D) + idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Accumulate weighted V for (int k = 0; k < actual_Bc; ++k) { float p = s_reg[k]; for (int i = 0; i < elem_per_thread; ++i) { int d = tid + i * WARP_SIZE; if (d < D) o_reg[i] += p * v_smem[k * D + d]; } } __syncthreads(); // before next tile loads K into smem } // Normalize for (int i = 0; i < elem_per_thread; ++i) { o_reg[i] /= l; } // Write output for (int i = 0; i < elem_per_thread; ++i) { int d = tid + i * WARP_SIZE; if (d < D) o_ptr[d] = o_reg[i]; } } ``` Wait, `l = l * exp_scale + tile_sum;` and `o_reg[i] *= exp_scale;` `tile_sum` and `s_reg[k]` are per-thread within the warp. But `warp_reduce_sum` made `dot` identical across all threads in the warp? Yes, because `__shfl_down_sync` broadcasts the final sum to lane 0, but other lanes don't have it unless we broadcast. We need to make sure `dot` is the same for all threads in the warp. `warp_reduce_sum` function: ```cpp __inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); } return val; } ``` In this implementation, after the loop, lane 0 has the sum. Other lanes have partial sums? No. `__shfl_down_sync(mask, var, delta)`: each thread gets value from `threadIdx.x + delta`. After first iteration (offset=16), lanes 0-15 get val from 16-31. Then they add. Then offset=8, etc. After the loop, lane 0 has the total sum. But lane 1 has sum of lanes 1-16? No. Wait, `__shfl_down_sync` for lane `i` returns value from lane `i+delta` (if in mask). So after offset=16, lane 0 gets lane 16. lane 1 gets lane 17. etc. Then after offset=8, lane 0 gets lane 8 (which already had sum of 8..15? No, because lane 8 got lane 24 in first step). Wait, let's trace: Initial val_i. offset=16: val_i = val_i + val_{i+16} offset=8: val_i = val_i + val_{i+8} -> now val_i = sum of 4 elements? Actually, for a power-of-2 warp, this standard reduction leaves the result in lane 0 *only if* we use `__shfl_xor_sync`, not `__shfl_down_sync`. With `__shfl_down_sync`, after all iterations, lane `i` contains the sum of elements from `i` to `i+31` (wrap around? No, down means higher lane index). Actually, for a downward butterfly: Iteration offset=16: lane i gets lane i+16. val[i] becomes val[i] + val[i+16] Iteration offset=8: lane i gets lane i+8. val[i] becomes val[i] + val[i+8] ... After offset=1: lane i gets lane i+1. val[i] becomes sum of all lanes from i to 31? Not exactly. Lane 0: gets lane 1 (which has sum of 1..2? No). This is confusing. The standard warp reduction uses `__shfl_xor_sync` to broadcast the result to all lanes. Example: ```cpp __inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) val += __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); return val; } ``` With `__shfl_xor_sync`, lane `i` gets value from lane `i ^ offset`. After the loop, all lanes have the total sum. Similarly for max: ```cpp __inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_max(float val) { for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) val = fmaxf(val, __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset)); return val; } ``` We should use `__shfl_xor_sync`. Then `dot` is identical in all threads in the warp. So `s_reg[k]` will be identical across the warp. Then `tile_max` computed via `fmaxf` across `s_reg[k]` is also identical. `m_new` identical. `exp_scale` identical. `tile_sum` identical. `l` identical. So no divergence, good. One issue: `blockDim.y = 32` means 32 warps. Max 1024 threads. A100 supports 1024 threads/block. Fine. But 32 warps per block. Each warp handles one query. `Br = 32`. `S=512`. So `512/32 = 16` blocks per head. Total blocks = `B * H * 16 = 4 * 24 * 16 = 1536`. This is a good number of blocks for A100 (108 SMs). 1536 blocks / 108 SMs = ~14 waves. Good occupancy. Wait, 1536 blocks is launched. Each block uses ~80KB SMEM. A100 has 164KB SMEM per SM. So at most 2 blocks per SM (if SMEM limit). Actually, 80KB means 2 blocks per SM = 216 blocks concurrently. Total 1536 / 216 = 7 waves. That's okay. Can we increase `blockDim.y`? If `Br=64`, threads = 2048 > 1024. Not allowed. So `Br=32` is max for 1D warps of 32 threads. Alternatively, we can use `threadIdx.x = 64` and `threadIdx.y=16` (1024 threads), splitting D across 64 threads. `elem_per_thread = D/64`. For D=128, `elem_per_thread=2`. Then one warp can handle 2 query tokens? This increases complexity but could allow `Br=16` or less. But `Br=32` with 1 thread per D-slice is fine. Let's optimize memory loads. `q_ptr` loads `elem_per_thread` floats per thread. For D=128, `elem_per_thread=4`. Coalesced? Thread 0 loads d=0, thread 1 loads d=1, etc. Yes, fully coalesced. Similarly, writing output is coalesced. Loading K tile: `tidx = warp_id * 32 + tid`. It iterates over `actual_Bc * D` elements. For `actual_Bc=32`, `D=128`, total = 4096 elements. `tidx` goes 0..1023. So `total_threads=1024`. Each thread loads 4 elements. Coalesced. Good. Loading V tile similarly coalesced. One potential issue: the `k_smem` and `v_smem` usage. We use `__syncthreads()` to ensure K is fully loaded before warps start reading. Then after reading K and computing scores, we load V. We need `__syncthreads()` before loading V to avoid races (warps still reading K while others load V into the same smem buffer? No, we separated `k_smem` and `v_smem`, so no race. But we can reuse smem to save memory. We used separate pointers `k_smem = smem; v_smem = smem + 32*D;` so no reuse issues. But we need `__syncthreads()` after loading V before reading it. Wait, we allocated dynamic shared memory for both K and V. But in the launch, we only specified one size. Let's specify total size as `2 * 32 * D * sizeof(float)`. The `extern __shared__ float smem[];` pointer maps to that. We must ensure `actual_Bc` never exceeds 32. What if D is not a multiple of 32? For D=128, it's fine. For D=192, `elem_per_thread = ceil(192/32) = 6`. 32*6 = 192. Fine. For D=64, `elem_per_thread = 2`. Fine. The loop `for (int i = 0; i < elem_per_thread; ++i) { int d = tid + i * WARP_SIZE; if (d < D) ... }` handles it. One correction: `blockDim.y` is the number of warps. If we set `blockDim.y = 32`, then `warp_id` goes 0..31. What if `S` is not a multiple of 32? Then `q = blockIdx.x * 32 + warp_id`. `q >= S` threads return. That's fine. Some warps in the last block are idle. Another issue: `warp_reduce_sum` and `warp_reduce_max`. We need to define them as `__device__ __forceinline__` before the kernel. Let's write the full CUDA source. Also, we need a launch configuration wrapper that checks dimensions and calls the kernel. ```cpp torch::Tensor fused_attention_forward(torch::Tensor query, torch::Tensor key, torch::Tensor value) { // query: [B, Hq, S, D] // key: [B, Hkv, S, D] // value: [B, Hkv, S, D] auto B = query.size(0); auto Hq = query.size(1); auto S = query.size(2); auto D = query.size(3); auto Hkv = key.size(1); auto out = torch::empty_like(query); dim3 block(32, 32); // 32 threads per warp, 32 warps dim3 grid((S + 31) / 31, Hq, B); // careful: (S + Br - 1) / Br. Br = blockDim.y = 32. // So grid.x = (S + blockDim.y - 1) / blockDim.y; int grid_x = (S + block.y - 1) / block.y; dim3 grid(grid_x, Hq, B); size_t smem_size = 2 * 32 * D * sizeof(float); fused_attention_kernel<<<grid, block, smem_size>>>( query.data_ptr<float>(), key.data_ptr<float>(), value.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, Hq, Hkv, S, D); return out; } ``` Check: `block` uses `blockDim` names. In the CUDA runtime call: `dim3 block(32, 32);` -> `block.x=32`, `block.y=32`. In the kernel, `q = blockIdx.x * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y`. So `grid.x = ceil(S / blockDim.y)`. Now, we need to make sure `query`, `key`, `value` are contiguous float tensors on CUDA. We can `.contiguous()` in Python. Let's write the `cpp_sources` string. Do not include `PYBIND11_MODULE`. Use `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`. ```python from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cmath> #define WARP_SIZE 32 __inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) val += __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); return val; } __inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_max(float val) { for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) val = fmaxf(val, __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset)); return val; } __global__ void fused_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ Q, const float* __restrict__ K, const float* __restrict__ V, float* __restrict__ O, int B, int Hq, int Hkv, int S, int D) { int b = blockIdx.z; int h = blockIdx.y; int q_tile = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int warp_id = threadIdx.y; int q = q_tile * blockDim.y + warp_id; if (q >= S) return; int kv_head = h / (Hq / Hkv); int q_head_offset = ((b * Hq + h) * S + q) * D; int kv_head_offset = ((b * Hkv + kv_head) * S) * D; const float* q_ptr = Q + q_head_offset; const float* k_ptr = K + kv_head_offset; const float* v_ptr = V + kv_head_offset; float* o_ptr = O + q_head_offset; int elem_per_thread = (D + WARP_SIZE - 1) / WARP_SIZE; float q_reg[8]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < elem_per_thread; ++i) { int d = tid + i * WARP_SIZE; if (d < D) q_reg[i] = q_ptr[d]; } extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* k_smem = smem; float* v_smem = smem + 32 * D; float m = -INFINITY; float l = 0.0f; float o_reg[8]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < elem_per_thread; ++i) o_reg[i] = 0.0f; float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)D); float s_reg[32]; for (int kv_start = 0; kv_start < S; kv_start += 32) { int actual_Bc = min(32, S - kv_start); int total_threads = blockDim.x * blockDim.y; int tidx = warp_id * WARP_SIZE + tid; #pragma unroll for (int idx = tidx; idx < actual_Bc * D; idx += total_threads) { k_smem[idx] = k_ptr[kv_start * D + idx]; } __syncthreads(); #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < actual_Bc; ++k) { float dot = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < elem_per_thread; ++i) { int d = tid + i * WARP_SIZE; if (d < D) dot += q_reg[i] * k_smem[k * D + d]; } dot = warp_reduce_sum(dot) * scale; int kv_pos = kv_start + k; if (kv_pos > q) dot = -INFINITY; s_reg[k] = dot; } float tile_max = -INFINITY; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < actual_Bc; ++k) { tile_max = fmaxf(tile_max, s_reg[k]); } float m_new = fmaxf(m, tile_max); float exp_scale = expf(m - m_new); m = m_new; float tile_sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < actual_Bc; ++k) { s_reg[k] = expf(s_reg[k] - m_new); tile_sum += s_reg[k]; } l = l * exp_scale + tile_sum; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < elem_per_thread; ++i) { o_reg[i] *= exp_scale; } #pragma unroll for (int idx = tidx; idx < actual_Bc * D; idx += total_threads) { v_smem[idx] = v_ptr[kv_start * D + idx]; } __syncthreads(); #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < actual_Bc; ++k) { float p = s_reg[k]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < elem_per_thread; ++i) { int d = tid + i * WARP_SIZE; if (d < D) o_reg[i] += p * v_smem[k * D + d]; } } __syncthreads(); } #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < elem_per_thread; ++i) { o_reg[i] /= l; } #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < elem_per_thread; ++i) { int d = tid + i * WARP_SIZE; if (d < D) o_ptr[d] = o_reg[i]; } } torch::Tensor fused_attention_forward(torch::Tensor query, torch::Tensor key, torch::Tensor value) { query = query.contiguous(); key = key.contiguous(); value = value.contiguous(); int B = query.size(0); int Hq = query.size(1); int S = query.size(2); int D = query.size(3); int Hkv = key.size(1); auto out = torch::empty_like(query); dim3 block(WARP_SIZE, 32); int grid_x = (S + block.y - 1) / block.y; dim3 grid(grid_x, Hq, B); size_t smem = 2 * 32 * D * sizeof(float); fused_attention_kernel<<<grid, block, smem>>>( query.data_ptr<float>(), key.data_ptr<float>(), value.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, Hq, Hkv, S, D); return out; } """ module = load_inline( name="custom_attn", cpp_sources="torch::Tensor fused_attention_forward(torch::Tensor query, torch::Tensor key, torch::Tensor value);", cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["fused_attention_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], verbose=False, ) ``` Wait, `cpp_sources` in `load_inline` expects the C++ declarations corresponding to the CUDA functions, or a string with both host and device code? Actually, `load_inline` takes `cpp_sources` for C++ files and `cuda_sources` for `.cu` files. Since our function is defined in `cuda_sources`, we can leave `cpp_sources` empty or with a dummy forward declaration? Let me check `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` docs. `load_inline(name, cpp_sources, cuda_sources=None, functions=None, extra_cuda_cflags=None, ...)` The `cpp_sources` can contain the function definitions too? Usually, if `cuda_sources` contains the definitions, `cpp_sources` can be left empty or contain C++ host code that calls them. But if the functions are in `.cu` files, the pybind11 module needs to link against them. `load_inline` combines all sources. I think it's fine to put the C++ host wrapper in `cuda_sources` since nvcc compiles `.cu` files as C++ with CUDA. The `functions` list tells it which functions to bind. But the bound functions must be C++ callable (not `__global__`). Our `fused_attention_forward` is a host function. So it should work. However, it's common to put the host wrapper in `cpp_sources` and the kernel in `cuda_sources`. But separating them means we need a declaration in `cpp_sources` if we call the kernel from there. If we put everything in `cuda_sources`, the compiler (nvcc) handles it. `cpp_sources` can be empty or omitted? The signature says `cpp_sources`. We can use `cpp_sources=""` and put all code in `cuda_sources`. Wait, `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` docs say: "cpp_sources, cuda_sources (str, str or list) – ... If the same function name is provided in both cpp_sources and cuda_sources an error will be raised." So we can put all C++ code (including kernel and host wrapper) in `cpp_sources` if we want, provided it's saved as `.cpp`. But for CUDA kernels, we need `.cu` compilation. Actually, if we only provide `cpp_sources`, it compiles with `c++`. If we provide `cuda_sources`, it compiles with `nvcc`. Host functions defined in `cuda_sources` are fine. Let's put everything in `cuda_sources` and leave `cpp_sources` as `""` or minimal. But `load_inline` might require `cpp_sources` to be non-empty? Let's try `cpp_sources=""`. Another issue: `torch::Tensor::data_ptr<float>()` requires the tensor to be `float32`. Ensure inputs are `float`. In our `ModelNew`, after the QKV projections, the tensors are `float32` (assuming model loaded in fp32). Monkey patching details: StarCoder2 model classes structure: ```python class Starcoder2ForCausalLM(nn.Module): def __init__(self, config): self.model = Starcoder2Model(config) self.lm_head = nn.Linear(config.hidden_size, config.vocab_size, bias=False) def forward(self, input_ids, ...): outputs = self.model(input_ids, ...) logits = self.lm_head(outputs[0]) return CausalLMOutputWithPast(logits=logits, ...) ``` `Starcoder2Model.forward` returns `BaseModelOutputWithPast` where `.last_hidden_state` is the hidden states. So `self.model(x).logits` -> wait, the `Model.forward` does `self.model(x).logits`. But `self.model` is `AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(...)`. So `self.model(x)` returns a `CausalLMOutputWithPast` object, and `.logits` extracts the logits. So `ModelNew.forward(x)` should also return `self.model(x).logits`. In our wrapper, we modify the internal `model.model.layers` (the `Starcoder2Model` instance inside `Starcoder2ForCausalLM`). Let's verify attribute names. For `Starcoder2ForCausalLM`, the inner model is `self.model`. This inner model has `self.layers`. In `__init__`: ```python class CustomAttention(nn.Module): def __init__(self, original): super().__init__() self.q_proj = original.q_proj self.k_proj = original.k_proj self.v_proj = original.v_proj self.o_proj = original.o_proj self.rotary_emb = original.rotary_emb self.num_heads = original.num_heads self.num_key_value_heads = original.num_key_value_heads self.head_dim = original.head_dim self.hidden_size = original.hidden_size # maybe try to get scaling self.attention_dropout = original.attention_dropout if hasattr(original, 'attention_dropout') else 0.0 def forward(self, hidden_states, attention_mask=None, position_ids=None, past_key_value=None, output_attentions=False, use_cache=False, cache_position=None, **kwargs): bsz, q_len, _ = hidden_states.size() query_states = self.q_proj(hidden_states) key_states = self.k_proj(hidden_states) value_states = self.v_proj(hidden_states) query_states = query_states.view(bsz, q_len, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1,2) key_states = key_states.view(bsz, q_len, self.num_key_value_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1,2) value_states = value_states.view(bsz, q_len, self.num_key_value_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1,2) kv_seq_len = key_states.shape[-2] cos, sin = self.rotary_emb(value_states, seq_len=kv_seq_len) query_states, key_states = apply_rotary_pos_emb(query_states, key_states, cos, sin) query_states = query_states.contiguous() key_states = key_states.contiguous() value_states = value_states.contiguous() attn_output = fused_attention_forward(query_states, key_states, value_states) attn_output = attn_output.transpose(1,2).contiguous() attn_output = attn_output.view(bsz, q_len, self.hidden_size) attn_output = self.o_proj(attn_output) return attn_output, None, past_key_value ``` Potential issue: `rotary_emb` function requires importing from `transformers.models.starcoder2.modeling_starcoder2`. The function signature in newer transformers is: `apply_rotary_pos_emb(q, k, cos, sin, position_ids=None, unsqueeze_dim=1)` Or even newer: `apply_rotary_pos_emb(q, k, cos, sin, unsqueeze_dim=1)`. The `Starcoder2Attention.forward` might compute `cos, sin = self.rotary_emb(value_states, seq_len=kv_seq_len)`. Wait, newer `rotary_emb` is `self.rotary_emb(value_states, position_ids)` or `self.rotary_emb(value_states, seq_len=kv_seq_len)`? Let's check. In `transformers` `modeling_starcoder2.py`: Line similar to: ```python cos, sin = self.rotary_emb(value_states, seq_len=kv_seq_len) query_states, key_states = apply_rotary_pos_emb(query_states, key_states, cos, sin) ``` This was in older `transformers`. In newer versions (4.39+), `rotary_emb` is initialized as `Starcoder2RotaryEmbedding(config)`. Its `__call__` signature might be `forward(self, x, seq_len)`. Or in very new versions, `cos, sin = self.rotary_emb(value_states, position_ids)` or `self.rotary_emb(value_states, seq_len=kv_seq_len, position_ids=position_ids)`. Another robust approach: Since `Starcoder2Attention` is the module, and it stores its weights, we can write our forward to closely mimic the original but handle `rotary_emb` robustly. We can try calling `self.rotary_emb(value_states, seq_len=kv_seq_len)`. If `TypeError`, we try `self.rotary_emb(value_states, position_ids)`. Or even simpler: `cos, sin = self.rotary_emb(value_states, seq_len=kv_seq_len)` because this is the standard signature for `RotaryEmbedding` in most `transformers` versions for StarCoder2.
